<li>Monitoring and maintaining fluid intake, if intracranial pressure is present then restrict the fluid intake</li>
<li>Observing the infusion site for anything abnormal that might occur</li>
<li>Observing drug side effects and taking appropriate measures</li>
<li>Using dim light in the environment and controlling noise</li>
<li>Regularly changing the patient's position to prevent pressure sores and control convulsions</li>
<li>Observing the vital signs</li>
<li>Two to four hourly tepid sponging</li>
<li>Ensure comfort by providing an electric fan if necessary</li>
<li>Maintaining general body and oral hygiene</li>
<li>Physiotherapy when condition improves at a later date</li>
</ul>
<p>Bishops Computers
Bishops Computers</p>
<p>Bishops Computers
Page 260 of 425
Bishops Computers</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="complications">Complications<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#complications" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>You should observe the patient carefully and report to the physician in charge the onset of one or more of the following complications:</p>
<ul>
<li>Meningococcal septicaemia</li>
<li>Hypostatic pneumonia</li>
<li>Peripheral circulatory shock</li>
<li>Adrenal damage</li>
<li>Central nervous system: Hydrocephalus, cranial nerve palsies, mental retardation, subdural effusions, deafness, blindness, epilepsy</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="prognosis">Prognosis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#prognosis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>If the condition is identified early and effectively treated, the mortality rate does not exceed 5%. However, if treatment is delayed the mortality rate is 30%, but those who survive usually have permanent brain damage.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="prevention-and-control">Prevention and Control<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#prevention-and-control" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Cases of meningococcal infection should be isolated and treated. As it is a communicable disease, identified cases should be reported or notified to the District Medical Officer of Health immediately for control measures.</p>
<p>The community should be informed of the importance of early reporting and treatment. The dangers of overcrowding should be stressed. Prophylactic penicillin should be given to household contacts. In suspected epidemic children, who are drowsy or have fits, a referral should be made for screening and further observations. Suspected outbreaks in schools should be investigated and surveillance maintained.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="whooping-cough-pertussis">Whooping Cough (Pertussis)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#whooping-cough-pertussis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>This is an infectious respiratory disease, which is characterised by an inflammation of the mucous membranes of the lungs, pharynx, trachea and bronchi. Thick, stringy mucus is produced which the child tries to expectorate by coughing. The attacks of coughing reach a peak of violence, ending in an inspiratory whoop.</p>
<p>The infection is caused by a type of bacterium called bordetella pertussis (or pertussis bacillus). It is a gram-negative aerobic bacillus which is transmitted either through direct contact with soiled fomites or through airborne droplets spread from the infected person. The incubation period is usually seven to 14 days but the infectivity period is from two days before the onset of cough and continues for up to four or even six weeks.</p>
<p>This is a worldwide disease common among children between the ages of two to three years reaching up to five years. In infants of up to one year, it presents in a very severe form with a very high mortality rate. Both sexes are equally affected.</p>
<p>Can you think of the clinical manifestations of whooping cough?</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="clinical-manifestations-of-whooping-cough">Clinical Manifestations of Whooping Cough<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#clinical-manifestations-of-whooping-cough" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The following are some of the main clinical features of whooping cough:</p>
<ul>
<li>Acute illness, with a slow onset of cough and fever resembling common cold (catarrhal stage), the catarrhal stage takes a period of approximately 14 days</li>
<li>The cough increases and manifests as spasms of cough while breathing out (expiration) followed by crowing or whooping while breathing in (inspiration)</li>
<li>In mild cases or in babies, the typical whoop may be missing</li>
<li>Young babies may be very ill or have attacks of not breathing (apnoea) resulting in cyanosis</li>
<li>Coughing attacks are always associated with vomiting and strings of sticky mucus usually hang down the sides of the mouth and are more common at night</li>
<li>The underneath and the sides of the tongue may be sore and ulcerated</li>
<li>The whoop can last several weeks and may recur every time the child has another acute respiratory infection</li>
<li>There is a high mortality rate in young babies</li>
<li>Leucocytosis (up to 100,000cm) and lymphocytosis are usually present and these are quite important in confirming the diagnosis</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="nursing-care-and-treatment">Nursing Care and Treatment<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#nursing-care-and-treatment" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Nurse the child on complete bed rest to prevent paroxysms of coughing. The room environment should be kept warm and humid. Oxygen therapy is given continuously or when the child is cyanosed. The child's nutritional status is maintained by giving small, frequent feeds and</p>
<p>when the child vomits, an attempt should be made to feed them again.</p>
<p>For a child with whooping cough you should maintain a fluid balance chart and observe the child's vital signs such as the temperature, pulse and respiration. Record any findings every two to four hours until the condition improves.</p>
<p>Give health education to the parents. Continue to maintain the child's personal hygiene.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="medical-management">Medical Management<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#medical-management" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Medical treatment includes Erythromycin 40mg/kg daily or Septrin 30mg/kg BD for two weeks. This can reduce the duration of infection if administered within the first week of the illness. Mild sedatives to keep the child quiet should be given, for example, chloral hydrate or Phenobarbitone (heavy sedation must be avoided).</p>
<p>Phenegram in a dose of 5mg in the morning and 10mg in the evening may reduce whooping and vomiting and ensure a good night's sleep. Antibiotics may be given to prevent secondary complications.</p>
<p>Can you think of the possible complications of whooping cough and how preventive measures can be put in place?</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="complications">Complications<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#complications" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Be aware in the course of providing nursing care that one or more of the following complications may occur:</p>
<ul>
<li>Pneumonia which should be treated with antibiotics</li>
<li>Encephalopathy including convulsions which can be alleviated by performing a lumbar puncture to relieve intra-cranial pressure</li>
<li>Surgical emphysema (gaseous distension) escaping into the mediastinum and into subcutaneous tissues</li>
<li>Sub conjunctival haemorrhage</li>
<li>Bronchiectasis and lung collapse</li>
<li>Otitis media</li>
<li>Marasmus (malnutrition)</li>
<li>Epistaxis</li>
<li>Inguinal hernia and rectal prolapse</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="prevention">Prevention<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#prevention" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The primary form of prevention is immunisation. The pentavalent vaccine is given in early childhood (infancy). Three injections are given at four week intervals. Good nutrition, especially breast feeding is also necessary. Children should be kept in well ventilated houses.</p>
<p>Prevent contact between small babies and children who have pertussis whenever possible. Children who have household contacts should receive a course of erythromycin.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="poliomyelitis">Poliomyelitis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#poliomyelitis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Poliomyelitis is an acute infectious viral disease of the anterior horn cells of the spinal cord and sometimes of the lower part of the brain. It occurs sporadically or is epidemiological and usually affects children under five but more commonly children under three. It is characterised by varying degrees of paralysis.</p>
<p>In 1925, there were a few cases of poliomyelitis among the children of the European settlers in Kenya. It was not until 1950 that polio was classified as a public health problem in this country, although it should be noted that there was solid immunity within the indigenous African population. From 1954, epidemics began to break out in three-year intervals until the 1970's. As a result, immunisation notifications and surveillance were stepped up.</p>
<p>WHO 1973, Bulletin 48 pp. 421, 429 and 543 obtainable from Kenya National Archive.</p>
<p>In Kenya just like anywhere in Africa, the poliovirus which is of three types, was very common and infected almost all adults at one stage during their childhood.</p>
<p>To develop immunity against each type of the poliovirus, a person has to have been infected or immunised against that particular type and this is one of the reasons why a child is given three doses of polio vaccine.</p>
<p>Poliomyelitis is a complication of a viral infection which is usually confined to the pharynx and gastro-intestinal tract, but in some cases there are no symptoms at all.</p>
<p>In about 1% of the patients the virus gains entry into the nervous system and settles in the motor cells of the anterior horn of the spinal cord or in the medulla oblangata.</p>
<p>Some factors, which contribute to the invasion of the nervous system include muscular exhaustion, tooth extraction, tonsillectomy,</p>
<p>Bishops Computers
Bishops Computers</p>
<p>injections and damaged nerve endings. The invasion finally leads to paralysis.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="pathophysiology">Pathophysiology<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#pathophysiology" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The causative micro-organism is poliovirus. There are three types of viruses. Type one is known as Brunhilde, which is commonly associated with paralytic illness. Type two is called Lansing and Type three is known as Leon. The latter two are less commonly associated with paralysis.</p>
<p>It has an incubation period of three to six days but can extend between three to 21 days. It has an infective period of three to 14 days. The virus spreads mainly through the oral route (gastrointestinal tract) and then spreads to the lymphatic system. It can also spread through droplets.</p>
<p>The Five F's: Fingers, Flies, Faeces, Fluids and Food. These are the five routes of entry. Can you think of the clinical manifestations of Poliomyelitis?</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="clinical-manifestations">Clinical Manifestations<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#clinical-manifestations" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The infection manifests itself in several ways such as:</p>
<ul>
<li>The patient presents with fever of 39-40 C</li>
<li>General malaise</li>
<li>Vomiting and headache</li>
<li>Painful and tender muscles follow this a few days later</li>
<li>Paralysis of one or more limbs occurs as the muscles become weakened</li>
<li>The paralysis of the respiratory muscles follows without the child developing any other illness (this is referred to as flaccid paralysis)</li>
<li>Only the motor system is affected but without sensory loss</li>
</ul>
<p>There are various types of poliomyelitis.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="paralytic-type">Paralytic Type<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#paralytic-type" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>This can be divided into spinal polio, where the virus attacks the anterior horn of the grey matter (newness) and bulbar polio, where the virus attacks the grey matter of the brain stem probably following a tooth extraction during an epidemic.</p>
<p>The patient normally shows signs of having the flu, usually complicated by pneumonia.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="non-paralytic-type">Non-Paralytic Type<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#non-paralytic-type" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The patient experiences muscle pain and stiffness. This type of polio can also change to a paralytic type as a result of any kind of stress, by IM injection, walking long distances and cold weather</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="diagnostic-investigations">Diagnostic Investigations<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#diagnostic-investigations" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>A lumbar puncture should be performed to exclude the possibility of meningitis. Cerebral Spinal Fluid is usually clear in colour. Both lymphocytes and polymorphs may be present in the CSF.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="how-would-you-manage-poliomyelitis">How would you manage poliomyelitis?<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#how-would-you-manage-poliomyelitis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="management-of-poliomyelitis">Management of Poliomyelitis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#management-of-poliomyelitis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>This can be divided into supportive and preventive management.</p>
<ul>
<li>The patient is strictly confined to bed</li>
<li>Activities in the first two weeks of the infection risk possible increased paralysis and should be avoided</li>
<li>The patient is to be nursed in isolation</li>
<li>Pain is controlled through the administration of analgesics, for example, paracetamol, valium or phenobarbitone</li>
<li>Regular respiratory suction and postural drainage should be performed</li>
<li>N.G tube feeding should be high calorie and include substantial amounts of protein</li>
<li>Change the patient's position every four hours to prevent bedsores</li>
<li>Surgical procedures should also be avoided</li>
<li>No injections are to be administered during this acute stage as they may precipitate paralysis</li>
</ul>
<p>Immobilise the affected limbs during the acute stage of the illness, using splints to prevent flexion deformities and promote rest.</p>
<p>After the acute stage has passed, begin gradual and gentle exercise of the affected limbs.</p>
<p>Bishops Computers
Bishops Computers</p>
<p>Ensure proper disposal of faeces and urine to prevent spread of infection. Urinary catheterisation must be passed but principles of asepsis must be observed strictly. Maintain an intravenous infusion and fluid balance chart. Oxygen therapy may be used when necessary. A tracheotomy and use of a mechanical respirator may be used should the patient's condition deteriorate.</p>
<p>After being discharged, the child should return to the clinic at regular intervals to ensure flexion deformities do not occur. A plaster of Paris or back slab should be applied to the limbs if these deformities actually occur. Special shoes and callipers may help severely affected children. Prolonged rehabilitation will be required at a later date when the patient is fully recovered.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="prevention">Prevention<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#prevention" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>If polio occurs in an area, the District Medical Officer of Health should be notified in writing immediately. Sabin oral vaccines are given to children to prevent them from getting poliomyelitis whenever there is an outbreak and routinely in MCH clinics. Parents should be encouraged to ensure their children are immunised.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="chickenpox-varicella">Chickenpox (Varicella)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#chickenpox-varicella" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="pathophysiology">Pathophysiology<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#pathophysiology" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>This is a mild viral infection, which is extremely contagious. Fever and a typical skin rash characterise it. The causative organism is Varicella Zoster Virus (VZV). It is spread in several ways. These include airborne droplet infection, direct or indirect contact, dry scabs and nut infections.</p>
<p>Droplets from the respiratory tract can transmit the Varicella Zoster Virus from one person to another. Even the wind is now known to transmit the virus particles from the skin of the infected person over a distance of meters to another person.</p>
<p>Once infected, the disease leaves immunity against chicken pox but the virus remains within the body and may reappear later in adult life as the herpes zoster when the person's immunity is weakened, for example in AIDS, diabetes, leukaemia and old age.</p>
<p>What length of incubation period would recommend for a patient suffering with VZV Incubation Period</p>
<p>It has an incubation period of ten to twenty one days. The period of communicability is about five days before the rash to six days after the appearance of the vesicles.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="clinical-manifestation">Clinical Manifestation<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#clinical-manifestation" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The infection presents itself in the following ways:</p>
<ul>
<li>Slight fever and sore throat are the first to appear in older children while younger children are affected by a skin rash</li>
<li>Maculopapular rash, which becomes vesicular within a few hours, appears on the trunk and spreads to the face, armpits, scalp and sometimes the extremities, (distribution to palms and soles is seldom)</li>
<li>Vesicles are usually superficial on the skin and in the mouth following the sensory nerve, groups of new pocks of rash will appear over many days</li>
<li>Pustules may form but usually the vesicles collapse and dry up after three to four days leaving no scars</li>
<li>Anorexia and headache may be present</li>
<li>Skin irritation (itching) and Lymphadenopathy are sometimes present</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="nursing-care">Nursing Care<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#nursing-care" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Confine the child to bed until the pyrexia settles down. Monitor the vital signs at regular intervals. The child requires plenty of fluids and a nourishing diet. The fingernails should be kept short and the child has to be restrained from scratching. General body cleanliness should be maintained. Soothing lotions such as Calamine, should be applied to the skin to soothe itching. Antibiotics are given prophylactically. Occasionally, the child may have to be sedated.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="complications">Complications<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#complications" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Complications include secondary infections of skin lesions. Pneumonia or encephalitis may also occur but are rare. Other possible complications may be thrombocytopenia, arthritis and nephritis.</p>
<p>Bishops Computers
Bishops Computers</p>
<p>Bishops Computers
Page 264 of 425
Bishops Computers</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="prevention-and-control">Prevention and Control<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#prevention-and-control" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>As part of active immunisation the live varicella vaccine is used selectively for immune suppressed children. The varicella zoster immune globulin is used for high risk individuals</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="measles">Measles<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#measles" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>This is an acute highly infectious disease. It is caused by the measles virus. Transmission is by droplet or direct contact with secretions from the nose and throat of infected persons. The child is most infectious to others during the prodromal phase, often before the diagnosis is made.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="incubation-period">Incubation Period<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#incubation-period" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Measles has an incubation period of seven to 14 days but usually 10 to 12 days is sufficient.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="isolation-period">Isolation Period<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#isolation-period" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The isolation period is five days after appearance of the rash.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="clinical-manifestations">Clinical Manifestations<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#clinical-manifestations" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Prodromal phase:</p>
<ul>
<li>This may last three to seven days</li>
<li>The first symptoms are runny nose, fever, conjunctivitis and coughing</li>
<li>There may be a faint rash which disappears quickly in the prodromal period</li>
<li>Koplik spots appear 24  48 hours before the main rash. Koplik spots are small white spots on a red base inside the cheeks, usually opposite the lower molars, but may occur on gums and inside lips as well</li>
</ul>
<p>Stage of advance:</p>
<p>The maculopapular rash starts behind the ears and on the forehead and spreads downwards. It takes about three days to reach the feet, at which point it starts to fade. Fever is high and lasts for four to five days.</p>
<p>What complications may occur with measles?</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="complications">Complications<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#complications" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ul>
<li>Otitis media</li>
<li>Respiratory infection</li>
<li>Pneumonia - This is usually a viral pneumonitis</li>
<li>Pulmonary TB</li>
<li>Kerato - conjunctivitis</li>
<li>Encephalitis is a serious complication often fatal or with residual brain damage</li>
<li>Gastroenteritis</li>
<li>Oral thrush and/or oral herpes</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="nursing-care-and-medical-management">Nursing Care and Medical Management<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#nursing-care-and-medical-management" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>There is no specific treatment available other than supportive. Supportive treatment includes:</p>
<ul>
<li>Antipyretics</li>
<li>Plenty of oral fluids</li>
<li>Eye and mouth hygiene</li>
<li>Vitamin A 200,000 units orally daily for two days</li>
<li>Check the child frequently for complication</li>
<li>Uncomplicated cases can be nursed at home but complicated cases, infants and malnourished children should be treated in a hospital with isolation facilities</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="prevention">Prevention<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#prevention" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Measles can be prevented through active immunisation with attenuated live virus vaccine. All children should be vaccinated against measles as per the KEPI schedule of immunisation.</p>
<p>The infection can be aborted if vaccine is given within 12 hours of exposure.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="mumps-infective-or-epidemic-parotitis">Mumps (Infective or Epidemic Parotitis)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#mumps-infective-or-epidemic-parotitis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>This is a viral infectious disease of the parotid glands that can also affect other glands. It can spread by droplets or contact with the salivary secretions of the infected person.</p>
<p>Bishops Computers
Page 265 of 425
Bishops Computers</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="incubation-period">Incubation Period<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#incubation-period" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The incubation period varies from patient to patient but is on average between 14 and 21 days of infectivity after the onset of the parotid glands swelling.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="what-symptoms-may-present-when-a-patient-contracts-the-mumps">What symptoms may present when a patient contracts the mumps?<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#what-symptoms-may-present-when-a-patient-contracts-the-mumps" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="clinical-manifestations">Clinical Manifestations<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#clinical-manifestations" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>All or some of the following symptoms may be present:</p>
<ul>
<li>The salivary glands namely the parotid, sublingual and submaxillary glands may be infected by painful swelling, this may be one side or both sides</li>
<li>The child develops fever and complains of headache and malaise</li>
<li>There is dysphagia (painful swallowing)</li>
<li>The tongue is furred and mouth dry due to diminished saliva</li>
<li>Moderate lymphocytosis is noted on blood examination</li>
<li>The tenderness may last two to three days then gradually subside</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="nursing-care">Nursing Care<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#nursing-care" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ul>
<li>Isolate during period of communicability</li>
<li>Maintain bed rest in a warm room until swelling subsides</li>
<li>Give analgesics and antipyretics as required</li>
<li>Encourage fluids and soft bland foods</li>
<li>Avoid foods which contain acid and which require chewing because they may increase pain</li>
<li>Apply heat or cold compress to neck whichever is more comfortable</li>
<li>Observe the child's vital signs of temperature, pulse and respiration and record them every four hours</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="prevention">Prevention<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#prevention" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Since the condition is caused by a virus, there is no specific drug treatment. However the active immunity of a live attenuated vaccine is available for those who are not already infected. The mumps virus vaccine is best given before puberty.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="what-possible-complications-may-arise-when-a-patient-has-mumps">What possible complications may arise when a patient has mumps?<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#what-possible-complications-may-arise-when-a-patient-has-mumps" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="complications">Complications<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#complications" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Complications after infections with the mumps virus are rare but you should be on the lookout for the following:</p>
<ul>
<li>The child may develop deafness</li>
<li>Inflammations of genital organs, such as the ovaries called oophoritis in girls and testes - orchitis in boys may occur, in both cases this may result in sterility in adulthood</li>
<li>Meningoencephalitis (inflammation of the meninges and brain)</li>
<li>Pancreatitis, which is inflammation of the pancreas</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="hepatitis">Hepatitis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#hepatitis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>This is the inflammation of the liver, most commonly caused by various types of viruses, namely A, B, C, D and E. In this subsection, you shall briefly look at Hepatitis A, B and C as they are related to paediatric illnesses.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="hepatitis-a-virus-hav-infectious-hepatitis">Hepatitis A Virus (HAV), Infectious Hepatitis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#hepatitis-a-virus-hav-infectious-hepatitis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>This virus usually occurs in epidemic form. It spreads from person to person by the faecal-oral route and ingestion of contaminated material. The virus is excreted in stools and urine from three weeks before to one week after the onset of clinical symptoms.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="incubation-period">Incubation Period<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#incubation-period" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>The incubation period is about three weeks.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="can-you-think-of-the-clinical-manifestations-of-hepatitis-a-virus">Can you think of the clinical manifestations of Hepatitis A Virus?<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#can-you-think-of-the-clinical-manifestations-of-hepatitis-a-virus" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="clinical-manifestations">Clinical Manifestations<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#clinical-manifestations" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>All or some of the following symptoms may be present:</p>
<ul>
<li>Gastro-intestinal upset (loss of appetite, nausea and vomiting)</li>
<li>Fever, headache, joint pains, tiredness</li>
<li>Jaundice</li>
<li>Clay-coloured stools, dark-brown urine</li>
<li>Enlarged and tender liver</li>
</ul>
<p>Bishops Computers
Page 266 of 425
Bishops Computers</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="investigations">Investigations<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#investigations" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ul>
<li>Serum bilirubin (increase of mainly direct bilirubin)</li>
<li>Liver function tests (abnormal)</li>
</ul>
<p>The disease is usually milder in children than in adults. Many cases of infectious hepatitis take a sub-clinical course, without jaundice there may be only mild symptoms such as slight fever and loss of appetite for a few days. Very occasionally, the disease is severe and may cause death due to acute liver failure.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="how-would-you-manage-hepatitis-a">How would you manage hepatitis A?<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#how-would-you-manage-hepatitis-a" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="the-management-of-hepatitis-a">The Management of Hepatitis A<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#the-management-of-hepatitis-a" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Most cases get better without treatment</li>
<li>Bed rest is usually recommended while jaundice is obvious</li>
<li>Hygienic disposal of stool and urine</li>
<li>Hand washing after contact with the patient</li>
<li>Free diet (but fatty foods are likely to be refused)</li>
<li>Refer patients to hospital if jaundice is very severe, vomiting persists and/or if confusion, coma or bleeding tendency occur</li>
<li>A vaccine is now available for contacts</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="hepatitis-b-hbv-serum-hepatitis">Hepatitis B (HBV) - Serum Hepatitis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#hepatitis-b-hbv-serum-hepatitis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Although a common infection on a global scale, it is more prevalent in sub-sahara Africa because of the high perinatal transmission rate and close contact between toddlers. It can also be spread through other routes. It is caused by the hepatitis B virus (HBV). The virus consists of a capsule and 'o' core, which contains DNA (deoxyribonucleic acid) and DNA polymerase enzymes. The virus circulates in the bloodstream. It can infect people of any age group.</p>
<p>The incubation period tends to be longer than that of other viruses, which affect the liver but usually in the range of 40 to 180 days. The communicability period is just a few days to one month during which period the individual has become a carrier. In many countries the carrier number is as high as 5-20%.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="how-many-ways-can-you-think-of-that-hbv-can-be-transmitted">How many ways can you think of that HBV can be transmitted?<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#how-many-ways-can-you-think-of-that-hbv-can-be-transmitted" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>There are several routes of transmission. These include:</p>
<ul>
<li>Trans-placental route, from mother to foetus through the placenta (vertical)</li>
<li>Blood transfusion, if the donor blood was not properly screened</li>
<li>Contact with other body fluids and secretions</li>
<li>Haemodialysis, especially children</li>
<li>Through injections with contaminated needles and broken skin surface</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="diagnostic-investigations">Diagnostic Investigations<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#diagnostic-investigations" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Diagnostic investigations include the examination of venous blood specimen in the laboratory when hepatitis B antigen/antibodies are found to be positive. Blood should be examined for bilirubin and alkaline phosphates but tests are not always conclusive. A liver function test may also be carried out but should be avoided in children.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="clinical-features-of-hepatitis-b">Clinical Features of Hepatitis B<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#clinical-features-of-hepatitis-b" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The clinical manifestations occur very slowly. These may begin with mild fever, anorexia, general malaise, nausea and vomiting. As the condition progresses, the patient will complain of abdominal discomfort. Occasionally mild jaundice may be present. Bile in the urine and a low white blood count may be noticed.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="nursing-care">Nursing Care<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#nursing-care" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>When nursing a child admitted to your ward with this condition, you should allow them to regulate their own activities. The diet should be high protein, high calorie, and high carbohydrate but low fat. Vomiting, which may be persistent ought to be managed by intravenous administration of fluids.</p>
<p>A fluid balance chart is maintained during this period. Precautionary measures, which include wearing gloves, when carrying out intimate procedures should be observed.</p>
<p>There is no specific drug treatment but certain antibiotics may be administered when there is an onset of complications.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="prevention-of-hepatitis-b">Prevention of Hepatitis B<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#prevention-of-hepatitis-b" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>In order to prevent spread of infections, you should make every effort to ensure that your hands are thoroughly washed after handling the patient, as well as all the articles, including linen used by the patient. The toilets should be cleaned with disinfectants.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="complications">Complications<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#complications" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Complications may emerge much later and may include:</p>
<ul>
<li>Acute fulminating hepatitis characterised by rapidly rising bilirubin</li>
<li>Encephalopathy, which is a degenerative process of the brain</li>
<li>Oedema and ascites will always be present in the advanced stage</li>
<li>Hepatic com-unconsciousness due to liver failure, chronic active hepatitis with hepatic dysfunction plus cirrhosis of the liver</li>
<li>In some cases cirrhosis of the liver may undergo malignant changes</li>
</ul>
<p>Nowadays, active immunisation with a hepatitis B vaccine is available. This is given in three doses. The first is followed by a second four weeks later. The third is administered six weeks thereafter.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="hepatitis-c">Hepatitis C<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#hepatitis-c" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>This type of virus has Ribonucleic Acid (RNA) in its nucleus. It causes hepatitis in a similar way to hepatitis B, although the risk to health care workers and sexual transmission is less marked. The main difference however, is its high rate of persistent infection, which increases the likelihood of the patient developing chronic hepatitis and cirrhosis of the liver.</p>
<p>It has an incubation period of approximately two to 26 weeks from the initial entry of the virus. It is transmitted in the same way as hepatitis B except that both sexual and vertical transmission are quite uncommon. There is no specific mode of prevention. Nursing care is the same as for hepatitis B.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="tuberculosis">Tuberculosis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#tuberculosis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Tuberculosis is an infectious respiratory disease, which affects the lungs and is spread by the droplet method. The infection is due to a rod-shaped micro-organism called tubercle bacillus. In some cases the infection may involve other body systems such as the genito-urinary, skeletal, gastro-intestinal or nervous system. Pathologically, it is characterised by an acute or chronic inflammatory process.</p>
<p>Many factors predispose to the development of tuberculosis. Children under the age of two years are more susceptible than older ones.</p>
<p>Although it is known that the condition is not hereditary, it is thought that tuberculosis is related to overcrowding in poorly ventilated accommodation, malnutrition and many other conditions, which lower the individual's immunity.</p>
<p>Tuberculosis is caused by tubercle bacillus. There are two distinct strains of the tubercle bacillus pathogenic to humans, namely the pulmonary tuberculosis which affects the lungs and the bovine type acquired through the drinking of infected animal milk.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260111-23247238.jpeg" alt="img-9.jpeg"></p>
<p>The pulmonary type of infection makes up to 90% of the total number of patients suffering from tuberculosis. The remaining 10% are extra-pulmonary and this condition affects other body organs as you have seen earlier. In developing countries, where there is a lack of facilities for pasteurisation of milk, the extra-pulmonary tuberculosis is just as common as that of the lungs.</p>
<p>Usually for children the most common approach to diagnostic investigations is history-taking, physical examination, including general health status and chest x-ray. A sputum specimen for AFB (acid fast bacilli) may be taken from older children. This is usually done three times. In very young patients, gastric aspirates are examined.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="clinical-features">Clinical Features<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#clinical-features" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ul>
<li>General malaise</li>
<li>Prolonged fever</li>
<li>Anorexia</li>
<li>Weight loss and local signs of infection</li>
</ul>
<p>Bishops Computers
Bishops Computers</p>
<ul>
<li>Chest x-ray film may show radiological changes</li>
<li>In some patients, pleural effusion may be present also to include lymphatic nodes enlargement</li>
</ul>
<p>What steps would you carry out in the nursing management of tuberculosis?</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="nursing-management">Nursing Management<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#nursing-management" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>When the diagnosis is confirmed, the parents should be consulted by the nurse and doctor to see whether admission to the ward is necessary or whether the child can be treated as an outpatient. If admitted, the mother should be encouraged to stay and help with the child's care. Nursing care should aim for infection control, bed rest and high protein diet with oral fluid intake. The patient's personal body hygiene should be maintained at all times.</p>
<p>If primary drug resistance is suspected, quadruple therapy should be given as initial treatment. Usually the child will be prescribed daily anti-tuberculous drugs, which you should administer continuously for a minimum of six months with at least two drugs. The drugs regimen can be selected from the following:</p>
<ul>
<li>Rifampicin 10-20mgs/kg</li>
<li>Isoniazid 10mgs/kg</li>
<li>Ethambutal 25mgs/kg</li>
<li>Pyrazinamide 20-35mgs/kg</li>
<li>Streptomycin sulphate 30mgs/kg</li>
</ul>
<p>The dose will be determined according to the child's age and weight. The drugs have to be administered daily in combination to prevent bacterial drug resistance to one particular drug. The drugs are very toxic and so the child should be observed very close!</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="nursing-management">Nursing Management<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#nursing-management" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>When the diagnosis is confirmed, the parents should be consulted by the nurse and doctor to see whether admission to the ward is necessary or whether the child can be treated as an outpatient. If admitted, the mother should be encouraged to stay and help with the child's care. Nursing care should aim for infection control, bed rest and high protein diet with oral fluid intake. The patient's personal body hygiene should be maintained at all times.</p>
<p>If primary drug resistance is suspected, quadruple therapy should be given as initial treatment. Usually the child will be prescribed daily anti-tuberculous drugs, which you should administer continuously for a minimum of six months with at least two drugs. The drugs regimen can be selected from the following:</p>
<ul>
<li>Rifampicin 10-20mgs/kg</li>
<li>Isoniazid 10mgs/kg</li>
<li>Ethambutal 25mgs/kg</li>
<li>Pyrazinamide 20-35mgs/kg</li>
<li>Streptomycin sulphate 30mgs/kg</li>
</ul>
<p>The dose will be determined according to the child's age and weight. The drugs have to be administered daily in combination to prevent bacterial drug resistance to one particular drug. The drugs are very toxic and so the child should be observed very close!</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="preventive-measures">Preventive Measures<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#preventive-measures" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>You should make every effort to prevent the spread of infections, primarily by ensuring that all children have received their BCG vaccination.</p>
<p>You should also give health education to members of the community so that the parents may bring their children to the clinic when infection is suspected and also avoid conditions that favour the disease</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="malaria">Malaria<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#malaria" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Malaria is the most common disease in the equatorial regions (tropical and sub-tropical). In some parts of Kenya, malaria is continuously endemic and young children are more susceptible than adults because of lack of resistance.</p>
<p>The infection is caused by a parasite called plasmodium and is transmitted from the infected person to an uninfected individual by the female anopheles mosquito bite. The male mosquito does not cause infection. The mosquito bites the infected person, draws blood into its stomach and when it bites the uninfected person it transfers the parasite contained in the blood to the victim.</p>
<p>There are four main types of parasites known in medical practice.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="plasmodium-falciparum">Plasmodium Falciparum<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#plasmodium-falciparum" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Malignant tertian malaria, which is sometimes referred to as Asian form of malaria</p>
<p>Bishops Computers
Bishops Computers</p>
<p>Bishops Computers
Page 269 of 425
Bishops Computers</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="plasmodium-malariae">Plasmodium Malariae<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#plasmodium-malariae" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Is the species which causes quartan malaria where fever usually occurs within 72 hours after an individual is infected.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="plasmodium-ovale">Plasmodium Ovale<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#plasmodium-ovale" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Which is a species, found predominantly in East and Central Africa.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="plasmodium-vivax">Plasmodium Vivax<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#plasmodium-vivax" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The species causing vivax malaria. This may be found in the Horn of Africa</p>
<p>All species of plasmodia have the same life cycle.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="life-cycle-within-the-human-body">Life Cycle Within the Human Body<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#life-cycle-within-the-human-body" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ul>
<li>Sporozoites present in the mosquito salivary glands, are injected into the human body, they very quickly circulate to the liver and remain in blood circulation for as long as 30 minutes or so.</li>
<li>During the liver or hepatic stage the sporozoite is taken up by the Kupffer cells of the liver then through the liver cells (hepatocyte). Here it develops into Liver Schizont where thousands of merozoites are released into the general blood circulation. This period takes approximately nine to 14 days.</li>
<li>In the Erythrocytic (RBC) stage the merozoites released from the liver attack individual Red Blood Cells (RBCs) and there develop inside them from the rings into Schizonts. RBCs are then ruptured by the schizonts releasing merozoites, which then invade the new RBCs. At this stage clinical signs of ill health are manifested.</li>
<li>Gametocytes develop at an unknown stage or time in the RBC cycle. The sexual forms, which develop are responsible for the survival and transmission of the parasite. These male and female gametocytes circulate for a few weeks and are taken up by the feeding mosquito.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="life-cycle-of-the-mosquito">Life Cycle of the Mosquito<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#life-cycle-of-the-mosquito" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ul>
<li>The male and female gametocytes develop into gametes and then fuse in the stomach of the mosquito to form zygote. These then develop into a mobile ookinete which migrates through the wall of the stomach to form an oocyst.</li>
<li>The oocyst matures and later ruptures to release sporozoites.</li>
<li>Sporozoites migrate to the salivary glands ready to be injected into the host bloodstream when the mosquito is ready for its next meal.</li>
</ul>
<p>Some people develop immunity to malaria, which occurs as a result of frequent exposure to malarial infections. The immunity may be partial.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="risk-groups">Risk Groups<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#risk-groups" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ul>
<li>Children who are under five years of age who have no or little immunity</li>
<li>Pregnant women are at risk because the malarial parasites flourish well in their placenta causing abortion or low birth weight and occasionally death of the foetus</li>
<li>Those children suffering from sickle cell anaemia may go into sickle cell crisis</li>
<li>Travellers or tourists from countries that do not have malaria because they have no immunity</li>
</ul>
